### [136] [Trivium: Temporal Regret as a First-Class Objective for Causal-Memory Controllers](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04421)
*Edward Y. Chang*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: Proposes a tri-regret framework (outcome, temporal, epistemic) for diagnosing and correcting long-horizon agent failures, plus Trivium and falsifiable predictions; shows outcome-only learning can fail under confounding, but with a persistent causal log and probes, regret scales as O(log E) or O(K log E); validated on CausalBench-Seq and initial real-LLM runs.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current systems optimize only outcome reward, neglecting the when/why of failure; structural miscalibration persists because failures are not logged/repaired beyond outcomes.

Method: Define three regret notions over a causal model and a stream of episodes; derive conditional guarantees under causal-probing, persistence, and detectability; instantiate a system named Trivium; pre-register five falsifiable predictions; evaluate on CausalBench-Seq and real-LLM pilots; clarify self-learning as updating external causal models, not LLM weights.

Result: Outcome-only learning cannot distinguish causal from spurious structure under observational confounding; temporal miscalibration can persist linearly. With a persistent causal log and budgeted probes, total probe complexity is O(log E); with K detectable change-points, O(K log E). Trivium follows the predicted logarithmic envelope; real-LLM pilot data show preliminary external-validity across E=500 and three E=100 pilots; comparisons show outcome-only baselines grow linearly.

Conclusion: A falsifiable, three-regret account clarifies what, why, and when long-lived agents fail; systematic logging and probing enable efficient correction via Trivium; results support logarithmic regret scaling under certain assumptions; further external validation and broader testing are needed.

Abstract: Many current agentic systems and LLM pipelines correct mistakes by optimizing outcome reward. This addresses only the what of failure: when an outcome diverges from prediction, the why and when of the mismatch are not systematically logged, reviewed, or corrected, so the same error can recur episode after episode. We argue that this is a structural problem, not merely a model-capacity one. We propose long-horizon temporal regret as a first-class objective alongside outcome regret and epistemic regret over the working causal model. Temporal regret captures when failure persists: how long a miscalibrated causal model is tolerated before correction. Epistemic regret captures why failure persists: residual uncertainty or error in the working causal model. Together, the three regrets give a falsifiable account of what, why, and when a long-lived agent can fail. Modeling the agent as a stream of E episodes, we prove three conditional results under explicit causal-probing, persistence, and detectability assumptions. First, under observationally equivalent confounding, outcome-only learning cannot distinguish causal from spurious structure without an intervention channel, so temporal miscalibration can persist linearly even after outcome regret is driven to zero. Second, with a persistent causal log and budgeted probes, total probe complexity is logarithmic in the episode horizon, inducing O(log E) temporal regret. Third, under K detectable change-points, the rate extends to O(K log E). We instantiate Trivium and pre-register five falsifiable predictions. On CausalBench-Seq, Trivium follows the predicted logarithmic envelope while outcome-only baselines grow linearly. A pilot real-LLM stream provides preliminary external-validity evidence across one full E = 500 run and three E = 100 frontier-model pilots. Self-learning here means revising an external causal model, not retraining LLM weights.

</details>


### [137] [AIP: A Graph Representation for Learning and Governing Agent Skills](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04781)
*Zachary Blumenfeld,Jim Webber*

Main category: cs.AI

TL;DR: AIP converts agent skills into a directed execution graph of nodes (steps) with deterministic or NL scripts, connected by typed I/O edges and validated by a YAML schema; this yields runnable units, easier diagnosis, and governance, with significant performance gains on SkillsBench tasks and a tunable improvement loop.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Address the fragility and inefficiency of free-form prose-based agent skills, enabling reliable execution, straightforward skill creation/improvement, and governance; also provide a natural space for RL over skills.

Method: Define an execution graph (nodes as steps) backed by deterministic scripts or NL descriptions, with typed input/output edges; enforce a schema-validated YAML specification; use a compiler meta-skill to translate existing human-written skills into AIP; evaluate on 27 real agent tasks from SkillsBench; compare metrics (mean task reward, pass rate) using nonparametric tests; diagnose failures at script level and iterate; discuss governance and RL implications.

Result: Mean task reward rose from 0.60 to 0.71; pass rate from 53% to 67% across 27 tasks; Wilcoxon p=0.011; 12 tasks won, 2 lost, 13 tied; often shorter wall-clock time; two authored-skill failures traced to scripts and fixed, yielding zero regressions (e.g., 0/5 to 5/5); enables a tuning loop and supports corpus-level governance and reinforcement learning over skills.

Conclusion: Modeling skills as schema-validated execution graphs yields more reliable, diagnosable, and evolvable agent capabilities; it reduces prose fragility, provides precise repair mechanisms, and creates governance and RL-friendly space for continual skill improvement; potential considerations include tooling overhead and scalability concerns, suggesting future work on automation and generalization.

Abstract: Agent Skills today consist largely of free-form prose requiring the agent to read, interpret, and re-derive how to act in every session. This imposes two compounding costs: reduced reliability on implementation-heavy tasks, and difficulty in skill creation and improvement, since editing prose is a fragile process that both humans and agents struggle with, particularly for domain-specific procedural knowledge underrepresented in model training. The Agent Instruction Protocol (AIP) addresses both by modeling a skill as a directed execution graph: discrete steps as nodes backed by deterministic scripts or natural-language descriptions, connected by explicit typed input/output edges, and governed by a schema-validated YAML specification. A compiler meta-skill translates existing human-written skills into this form. The benefits are twofold. First, compiling human-written skills to AIP raised Claude Sonnet's mean task reward from 0.60 to 0.71 and pass rate from 53% to 67% across 27 real agent tasks from SkillsBench - a statistically significant gain (Wilcoxon signed-rank p = 0.011), winning 12 tasks to 2 with 13 ties - often in less wall-clock time. The graph delivers vetted, runnable units to the agent rather than asking it to re-derive code, commands, and tool calls from natural language. Second, on creation and improvement, because each skill is schema-validated, functionally testable, and addressable node-by-node, failures can be diagnosed and repaired precisely. Two authored-skill failures were traced to the script level. After adjusting the AIP spec and recompiling, both recovered with zero regressions (one task going from 0/5 to 5/5), turning skill improvement into a measurable tuning loop rather than a prose rewrite. That same graph structure supports corpus-level governance and skill introspection, and provides a natural action space for reinforcement learning over skills.

</details>

### [141] [CoPark: Learning Reactive Parking via Self-Play](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04149)
*Jiarong Wei,Yanxing Chen,Sinuo Song,Yin Wu,Anna Rehr,Abhinav Valada*

Main category: cs.RO

TL;DR: CoPark integrates a fixed offline plan with a residual RL head for reactive multi-agent autonomous parking, using partner-threat-modulated channel control and a refinement loop to balance precise slot alignment with safe, responsive interaction; achieves 70-85% success and 3-6% collisions, with emergent yielding behaviors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Achieve sub-meter precision parking in multi-agent settings while maintaining safety and responsiveness to nearby vehicles, a conflict between rigid precision and dynamic interaction.

Method: A residual-policy architecture: an offline fixed action prior from a precomputed plan plus a trainable residual head that learns reactive corrections. Self-play data fuels the residual policy; a partner-threat-modulated, channel-asymmetric mechanism shifts control authority from the fixed prior to the residual head in the longitudinal channel under threat, while preserving lateral alignment with the prior. A closed-loop refinement layer compensates action-grid discretization errors. Trains on six parking lots; evaluates zero-shot on a new benchmark (DLP and DSC3D).

Result: CoPark attains approximately 70-85% success with 3-6% collision rate, outperforming classical, imitation-learning, and large-scale RL baselines. Demonstrates emergent interaction behaviors such as reverse-yielding, mid-maneuver yielding, tight-corridor passing, and queuing.

Conclusion: A hybrid planning-learned policy that decouples longitudinal responsiveness from lateral precision via threat-modulated control yields robust, sub-meter parking performance in multi-agent scenarios and supports emergent, cooperative behaviors. The approach highlights the value of combining precomputed plans with learnable residuals and self-play for safety-critical, interactive tasks.

Abstract: Learning a single policy that reaches a goal with high geometric precision while interacting safely with nearby agents poses conflicting objectives. Precision favors commitment to a fixed geometric plan, whereas interaction requires immediate deviation when another agent intrudes, causing policies optimized for one objective to often fail at the other. We study this problem in the context of reactive autonomous parking, where multiple vehicles must reach assigned slots with sub-meter terminal accuracy while remaining responsive to neighboring vehicles throughout the maneuver. We propose CoPark, a multi-agent self-play RL approach built on a residual-policy architecture. A precomputed offline plan provides a fixed action prior, while a residual head learns the reactive corrections. The residual policy learns behaviors under self-play, where data and scripting fall short, while the fixed prior holds the slot-frame geometry that pure policies struggle to reach reliably. The key design is a partner-threat-modulated, channel-asymmetric release of the prior. A continuous threat signal shifts authority of the longitudinal channel to the residual head to enable yielding, while the lateral channel remains anchored to the precomputed reference to preserve sub-meter slot alignment. A closed-loop refinement layer corrects residual terminal error from action-grid discretization. We train our policy on six parking lots and evaluate zero-shot on our new reactive-parking benchmark spanning Dragon Lake Parking (DLP) and DeepScenario Open 3D (DSC3D). CoPark achieves ~70-85% success with only 3-6% collision rate, substantially outperforming classical, imitation-learning, and large-scale RL baselines. Importantly, the results demonstrate emergent interaction behaviors such as reverse-yielding, mid-maneuver yielding, tight-corridor passing, and queuing.

### [143] [Multi-Agent Next-Best-View Optimization for Risk-Averse Planning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04158)
*Amirhossein Mollaei Khass,Vivek Pandey,Guangyi Liu,Athanasios Cosse,Emrah Bayrak,Nader Motee*

Main category: cs.RO

TL;DR: Distributed, risk-aware multi-agent NBV using private 3D Gaussian Splatting maps solved with Consensus ADMM; achieves near-central performance with substantially reduced communication.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scale NBV planning to multiple agents in uncertain/unknown environments without overloading communication; centralized approaches require sharing raw data, limiting scalability and safety.

Method: Each agent maintains a private 3D Gaussian Splatting (3DGS) map. The team jointly maximizes expected information gain (EIG) restricted to masked zones along planned trajectories. A distributed objective is solved via Consensus ADMM (C-ADMM) over a communication graph, with agents exchanging only candidate viewpoints, trajectory descriptors, and scalar EIG contributions. Collision risk is modeled with Average Value-at-Risk (AV@R) over local 3DGS maps to shape masking radii and score trajectories.

Result: In Gibson environments with varying team sizes, the distributed approach approaches the centralized baseline in mapping quality and trajectory safety while reducing communication by orders of magnitude.

Conclusion: The proposed distributed risk-aware NBV framework offers scalable, efficient coordination that matches centralized performance while drastically lowering communication load; validates distributed C-ADMM-based optimization with AV@R-based risk shaping.

Abstract: Multi-agent Next-Best-View (NBV) selection for safe path planning in uncertain and unknown environments requires informative, safety-aware, and efficient coordination. Centralized approaches rely on sharing raw sensor data or significant communication overhead, resulting in limited scalability. We propose a distributed, risk-aware multi-agent NBV framework in which each robot maintains a private local 3D Gaussian Splatting map and the team jointly maximizes expected information gain (EIG) restricted to masked zones along planned trajectories. The resulting distributed objective is solved by Consensus ADMM (C-ADMM) over a communication graph, with each robot exchanging only candidate viewpoints, planned trajectory descriptors, and scalar EIG contributions. Collision risk along each trajectory is modeled via Average Value-at-Risk (AV@R) over the local 3DGS map and used both to shape the masking radius and to score planned paths. Experiments in Gibson environments at multiple team sizes show that the distributed formulation approaches the centralized baseline in mapping quality and trajectory safety while reducing communication by orders of magnitude.

To generate the prediction sets, we develop a distribution-free risk quantification framework that extends conformal risk control (CRC) to general spectral risk measures. We then show that incorporating the prediction sets into the MPC framework provides statistical safety guarantees in terms of spectral risk constraint satisfaction even under uncertainty misspecification. Method: Use an artificial residual-limb to provide a global wrench and instrumented clusters (four capacitance channels each) to obtain local normal and shear pressures; evaluate a quasi-static spring–mass contact model; identify model parameters via a two-stage convex least-squares problem; perform validation under static loading; include constant bias terms to reduce offsets; conduct Pareto-front sensitivity analysis comparing global vs local objective balances.

Result: Estimating constant bias terms reduces steady offsets in wrench measurements and improves agreement with local measurements; Pareto-front analysis reveals how the trade-off between global and local objectives changes when bias terms are included, guiding model selection for sparse sensing.

Conclusion: A practical framework to evaluate model-based augmentation of sparse contact sensing at the residual-limb/socket interface. Abstract: Prosthetic socket fitting remains largely manual and iterative, and objective fit metrics are still limited. Part of the challenge is the lack of long-term real-life pressure data at the residual limb--socket interface. Traditional pressure sensors are prone to drift over time, and capture only normal pressures at sparse locations within the socket, missing a critical component for biomechanical analysis: shear. Although some sensors can report both normal and shear interface stresses, these components are often difficult to decouple because of measurement crosstalk. One potential path forward is to develop models that can augment available measurements. This work introduces a testbed to evaluate model performance under sparse pressure sensing using two complementary validation signals: (i) the global wrench (\ie, total forces and moments expressed in an orthonormal frame) transmitted through the socket, by an artificial residual-limb, and (ii) local interface loads (\ie, decoupled normal and shear pressure components in a right-hand-rule orthogonal frame that lives in each instrumented location) measured by sparse sensing clusters, each composed of four capacitance-sensing channels. Rather than presenting full-field pressure estimates, the focus is on an analysis sequence that quantifies how well candidate mechanical models explain both global and local measurements under controlled conditions. A quasi-static spring--mass contact model is evaluated, and its parameters are identified via a two-stage convex least-squares problem. Validation under static loading shows that estimating constant bias terms reduces steady offsets in the wrench channels and improves agreement with local measurements. A Pareto-front sensitivity analysis further illustrates how the trade-off between global and local objectives changes when bias terms are included.

*Tianchong Jiang,Xiangshan Tan,Samuel Wheeler,Luzhe Sun,Tewodros W. Ayalew,Matthew Walter*

Main category: cs.RO

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Introduce one diagnostic per failure mode and apply them to a set of benchmarks (LIBERO, CALVIN, SimplerEnv, RoboCasa, RoboTwin 2.0). Evaluations include probing with a language-free 0.09B model, randomized block poses within training ranges, and other diagnostic checks to test statistical significance and data-source dependence. The authors provide reference implementations to enable authors/reviewers to apply these diagnostics.

Result: LIBERO and CALVIN fail multiple diagnostics; RoboCasa and RoboTwin 2.0 fail fewer diagnostics though they are less prominent in progress claims. Some reported SOTA results (e.g., a 0.09B probe) do not meet significance or robustness criteria. CALVIN shows performance drops when block poses are randomized within training ranges across tested policies.

Conclusion: The authors release four diagnostics with reference implementations to prevent benchmark scores from being treated as evidence of general progress; code and artifacts are publicly available for use by researchers and reviewers.

Abstract: A robotics benchmark score measures success under one fixed evaluation setup, yet is routinely treated as evidence of general manipulation capability. We identify four failure modes, each of which weakens or invalidates a benchmark's role as a valid proxy for that capability: shortcut solvability, lack of statistical significance, creeping overfitting, and data-source dependence. We propose one diagnostic per failure mode. We audit LIBERO, CALVIN, SimplerEnv, RoboCasa, and RoboTwin 2.0 under these diagnostics. LIBERO and CALVIN fail multiple diagnostics. RoboCasa and RoboTwin 2.0 fail fewer, despite appearing far less often in recent progress claims. On LIBERO, a 0.09B probe with no language encoder scores at or near reported SOTA, and most reported gains are not provably statistically significant. On CALVIN, randomizing block poses within the training range drops performance for every tested policy. We release the four diagnostics with reference implementations for authors and reviewers to apply before treating a benchmark score as evidence of progress. Code and artifacts are available at https://ripl.github.io/manipulation_benchmark_audit/.

TL;DR: ROP-RAS3 introduces online sampling-based macro actions to bias belief-space planning in long-horizon POMDPs, converging to a near-optimal reference-based solution with rate tied to sampled actions; empirically outperforms state-of-the-art on very long-horizon tasks and is demonstrated on a physical robot.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Long-horizon POMDPs are computationally intractable with exhaustive action-space exploration; there is a need for fast online planning that can generate diverse macro actions to guide belief-space search and improve success rates.

Method: Propose ROP-RAS3, which uses extremely fast sampling-based motion planning to sample the state space and online generate diverse macro actions; these actions bias belief-space sampling to infer high-quality policies without enumerating the full action space; converges to a near-optimal reference-based solution; builds on and extends ISRR24 work; code available.

Result: Evaluated on long-horizon POMDPs with up to 3000 lookahead steps and 35-dimensional state spaces, supporting continuous, discrete, and hybrid spaces; shows up to multiple-fold improvements in success rate over state-of-the-art methods; includes a physical robot demonstration.

Conclusion: Demonstrates scalable online POMDP planning for very long horizons via reference-based sampling; effective in practice and improves success rates, though the reference-based solution may differ from the true POMDP optimum; code is publicly available.

Abstract: Partially Observable Markov Decision Processes (POMDPs) are a general and principled framework for motion planning under uncertainty. Despite tremendous improvement in the scalability of POMDP solvers, long-horizon POMDPs remain difficult to solve. To alleviate the difficulty, this paper proposes a new approximate online POMDP solver, called Reference-Based Online POMDP Planning via Rapid State Space Sampling (ROP-RAS3). ROP-RAS3 uses novel extremely fast sampling-based motion planning techniques to sample the state space and generate a diverse set of macro actions online, which are then used to bias belief-space sampling and infer high-quality policies without requiring exhaustive enumeration of the action space -- a fundamental constraint for modern online POMDP solvers. ROP-RAS3 converges to a near-optimal reference-based solution at a rate that depends on the number of sampled actions, rather than the size of the action space. ROP-RAS3 is evaluated on various long-horizon POMDPs with up to 3000 lookahead steps and 35-dimensional state spaces, where the state, action and observation spaces can be continuous, discrete, or a hybrid of discrete and continuous. Although the reference-based optimal solution may not be the same as the optimal POMDP solution, empirical results indicate that in all of these problems, in terms of success rate, ROP-RAS3 outperforms other state-of-the-art methods by up to multiple folds. We also demonstrate the capability of our approach on a physical robot demonstration. This work extends the theory and empirical results of our ISRR24 paper. Code can be found at \texttt{https://github.com/RDLLab/ROPRAS3}.

TL;DR: Open-source, MuJoCo-based underground navigation benchmark (MineXplore) derived from real mine data, with high geometric fidelity, LiDAR-based wall geometry, lighting, and terrain variation; baseline PPO demonstrates learnability with robust, reproducible results but not all seeds reach 90% coverage.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There is a need for realistic, open benchmarks for autonomous navigation in GPS-denied, loop-rich underground environments to enable reproducible RL research and benchmarking with GPU-accelerated pipelines.

Method: An end-to-end pipeline (six-stage contour-to-MJCF) reconstructs a ~104,423 m^2 tunnel network with octagonal walls, LiDAR-derived jagged geometry, terrain friction zones, a global 5° incline, and periodic lighting in MuJoCo; validation includes IoU against survey maps and texture similarity scores; a single-agent PPO baseline trained with RLlib across five seeds.

Result: IoU with source map = 0.9538; surface texture similarity across six dimensions = 79.4%; best rolling coverage achieved = 88.89% (3/5 seeds >= 90%), indicating stable learning and reproducibility.

Conclusion: MineXplore provides a realistic, open-source benchmark platform that supports reproducible policy learning for underground navigation under challenging sensing and topology, bridging a gap in simulation benchmarks for production mines.

Abstract: Underground mines present extreme conditions for autonomous robot navigation: GPS is denied, lighting is degraded, and tunnel topology is loop-rich and non-convex. Simulation benchmarks grounded in real production-mine geometry and compatible with GPU-accelerated learning pipelines do not yet exist in the open-source ecosystem. We present MineXplore, an open-source MuJoCo-based navigation benchmark derived from the Leung et al. 2017 Chilean underground copper mine dataset. The environment reconstructs a 104,423 sq.m tunnel network through an six-stage contour-to-MJCF pipeline incorporating octagonal wall cross-sections, LiDAR-sourced jagged wall geometry, three terrain friction zones, a global 5 degree incline, and periodic spot lighting. Geometric fidelity is validated at an Intersection over Union (IoU) of 0.9538 against the source survey map, and surface texture similarity scores 79.4% across six structural dimensions. A single-agent PPO baseline trained via RLlib across five independent random seeds achieves a best rolling coverage of 88.89% (3 of 5 seeds reaching the 90% coverage target), confirming that MineXplore supports stable and reproducible policy learning under realistic underground sensing and topology.

### [156] [BPDA-GMM: Bayesian Probabilistic Data Association via Gaussian Mixture Models for Semantic SLAM](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04618)
*Thanh Nguyen Canh,Haolan Zhang,Xiem HoangVan,Antonio Sgorbissa,Nak Young Chong*

Main category: cs.RO

TL;DR: BPDA-GMM introduces an online, nonparametric Bayesian data association framework for semantic SLAM with a growing object-level map. It uses a Dirichlet-process prior to model CRP-based associations, enabling new landmark creation while favoring existing ones, and integrates α-divergence tempering and a decoupled back-end to improve robustness to perceptual aliasing and classifier errors.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Semantic SLAM systems with fixed landmark sets struggle with perceptual aliasing and do not scale as the map grows. A principled, online nonparametric data association that can grow the landmark set and automatically balance existing versus new landmarks is needed.

Method: A Dirichlet-process prior induces a Chinese Restaurant Process (CRP) for landmark associations. For each semantic detection, candidate landmarks are selected via a joint semantic-geometric gate; CRP-weighted association probabilities are computed; landmarks are updated as semantic Gaussians in closed form. The landmark set forms a Gaussian mixture model; the dominant component is used as a max-mixture semantic factor for the back-end. When associations are uncertain, an α-divergence tempering step enhances discrimination. The back-end decouples by zeroing the pose Jacobian of semantic factors, allowing noisy detections to refine landmarks without perturbing the trajectory.

Result: Simulation and real indoor experiments show improved trajectory accuracy, higher-quality semantic maps, and robustness to perceptual aliasing and classifier errors compared with state-of-the-art baselines. Code and video are publicly available.

Conclusion: BPDA-GMM provides a principled online, nonparametric framework for semantic SLAM with growing landmarks, integrating CRP-based data association, closed-form semantic Gaussian updates, and a back-end decoupling strategy to enhance robustness and accuracy in perceptually aliased environments.

Abstract: Probabilistic data association (PDA) improves semantic SLAM in perceptually aliased scenes, but existing methods often assume a fixed landmark set, recompute association weights as the map grows, or rely on hand-tuned null-hypothesis weights. To address these limitations, we propose \textbf{BPDA-GMM}, an online Bayesian PDA framework for semantic SLAM with a growing object-level map. BPDA-GMM uses a Dirichlet-process prior to induce a Chinese Restaurant Process (CRP) association model, where accumulated evidence favors existing landmarks, and the concentration parameter assigns probability mass to new landmarks. For each semantic detection, plausible candidates are selected by a joint semantic-geometric gate, CRP-weighted association probabilities are computed, and object landmarks are updated as semantic Gaussians in closed form. The resulting landmark set forms a Gaussian mixture model, and its dominant component is passed to the back-end as a max-mixture semantic factor. When association weights are inconclusive, an ambiguity-triggered $α$-divergence tempering step improves discrimination. Finally, a decoupled back-end zeroes the pose Jacobian of semantic factors, allowing noisy detections to refine landmarks without directly perturbing the trajectory. Experiments in simulation and on a real indoor dataset demonstrate improved trajectory accuracy, semantic mapping quality, and robustness to perceptual aliasing and classifier errors over state-of-the-art baselines. Code and video are publicly available at https://github.com/thanhnguyencanh/BPDA-SLAM.

</details>

*Kailun Huang,Zikang Xie,Yanzhe Xie,Panpan Liao,Fanghai Zhang,Yanheng Mai,Wenhao Xu,Yunheng Wang,Renjing Xu,Haohui Huang*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Unifying gait-transition and multi-terrain adaptation is hard due to gradient interference and distribution shifts caused by terrain-dependent visuals and dynamics; naive end-to-end MoE training often fails to specialize.

Similarly, humanoid robots should achieve smooth transitions between walking and running while maintaining natural and stable locomotion. However, unifying gait transition and multi-terrain adaptation within a single policy remains challenging due to gradient interference and the distribution shift induced by terrain-dependent visual and dynamic variations. Although Mixture-of-Experts (MoE) architectures can alleviate multi-skill interference, naive joint training often fails to yield clear expert specialization, limiting their effectiveness. To address these challenges, we propose CoRe-MoE, a two-stage reinforcement learning framework that decouples gait generation from terrain adaptation. In the first stage, a stable locomotion policy is learned to produce natural walking and running behaviors with smooth transitions. In the second stage, a terrain-aware MoE branch is introduced and trained with a contrastive objective to shape the gating network, enabling it to capture structured terrain representations and promote expert specialization. The final action is obtained via weighted fusion of the base gait policy and the terrain-aware branch, allowing the policy to preserve stable locomotion patterns while adapting to complex terrains. Extensive simulation results demonstrate that the proposed method outperforms baseline approaches in terms of success rate, locomotion stability, and multi-terrain adaptability. ### [158] [CADENCE: Predicting Realized MAPF Execution Time Beyond Sum of Costs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04746)
*Abhishek S,Badrikanath Praharaj,Sreeram MV*

Main category: cs.RO

TL;DR: CADENCE shows that primitive motion burden is the strongest offline predictor of wall-clock finish time for MAPF plans; SoC helps but is incomplete, while interaction-aware coordination adds modest gains. The offline plan features largely account for execution time gaps observed in hardware experiments.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Standard MAPF metrics (Sum of Costs, makespan, planner runtime) do not reliably predict real-world execution time; identifying pre-execution features that forecast wall-clock completion can bridge the gap between offline planning evaluation and hardware performance.

Method: Hardware study on a fixed 7x7 workcell with seven differential-drive robots. Generated 120 plans across 15 scenarios (5 Empty, 5 Medium Random, 5 Bottleneck) and executed each plan four times (480 trials). Compared four predictor features: SoC, total planned travel cost, primitive motion burden (e.g., makespan, turns, start-stop transitions), and interaction-aware coordination structure (dependency links, interacting robot pairs, dependency depth, crowding exposure). Built two models: a scenario-held-out ridge model and a trial-level mixed-effects model.

Result: SoC is informative but incomplete. Primitive motion burden provides the strongest predictive gain, reducing held-out MAE by ~48.6–59.8% and RMSE by ~44.2–61.4% over SoC-only baselines. Interaction-aware coordination features yield smaller, less consistent improvements, mainly in the mixed-effects analysis. Across models and uncertainty checks, primitive motion burden is the most reliable additional signal.

Conclusion: Most of the execution-time gap can be anticipated from offline plan features, particularly primitive motion burden, suggesting that offline plans already reveal much of the dynamics governing wall-clock performance.

Abstract: Multi-Agent Path Finding (MAPF) algorithms are increasingly used to plan motion for robot teams in industrial warehouses and robotic shared workspaces, but standard MAPF algorithm evaluation metrics, such as Sum of Costs (SoC), makespan, and planner runtime, can obscure how planner choices translate into realistic execution performance. We present CADENCE (Coordination and Action-Driven Estimation for Networked Continuous Execution), a hardware study of this evaluation gap on a fixed 7 by 7 workcell with seven differential drive robots, asking which features available before execution can best predict final wall-clock completion time. We compare SoC, total planned travel cost, primitive motion burden (how much basic motion the plan requires, such as makespan, turns, consecutive moves, and start-stop transitions), and interaction aware coordination structure (how much inter-robot coordination the plan induces, such as dependency links, interacting robot pairs, dependency depth, and crowding exposure). To test this, we generate 120 plans across 15 scenarios -- 5 Empty, 5 Medium Random, and 5 Bottleneck and execute each plan four times, yielding a 480 trial hardware corpus. Using both a scenario-held -- out ridge model and a trial-level mixed-effects model, we find that SoC alone is informative but incomplete, while primitive motion burden gives the strongest improvement, reducing held out error by about 48.6%-59.8% in MAE and 44.2%-61.4% in RMSE relative to SoC-only models. Interaction-aware coordination features add smaller, less uniform gains, most clearly in the mixed-effects analysis. Across both models and uncertainty checks, primitive motion burden is the most reliable additional signal beyond SoC, suggesting that much of the execution time gap is already visible in the offline plan before any robot starts moving.

### [159] [COP-Q: Safety-First Reinforcement Learning for Robot Control via Cholesky-Ordered Projection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04749)
*Guopeng Li,Moritz A. Zanger,Matthijs T. J. Spaan,Julian F. P. Kooij*

Main category: cs.RO

TL;DR: COP-Q proposes a Cholesky-ordered projection Q-learning method that uses inter-objective covariance to compute a joint confidence bound for safety and reward Q-values, reducing conservatism on reward while preserving safety.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Conservatism from independent treatment of safety and reward Q-values leads to inefficient learning; exploit cross-objective covariance to improve sample efficiency and safety trade-offs.

Method: Construct a generalized confidence bound in joint Q-value space using Cholesky factorization to encode objective priority; sequentially apply in TD target computation and actor update; compatible with deep Q-learning frameworks; minimal overhead.

Result: Empirical evaluation on Brax robot locomotion and Safety-Gymnasium safe navigation shows COP-Q achieves strong safety performance and competitive or improved sample efficiency across hard and soft safety settings compared to baselines.

Conclusion: Incorporating inter-objective covariance via COP-Q yields safer yet less conservative value estimates with practical efficiency and broad applicability.

Abstract: Safe robot control requires maximizing return while satisfying safety constraints. In off-policy safe reinforcement learning, reward and safety Q-values are commonly learned by separate critic ensembles, with uncertainty handled independently for each objective. This objective-wise treatment neglects inter-objective correlation and can lead to overly conservative value estimates, thereby reducing sample efficiency. To address this issue, we propose Cholesky-Ordered Projection Q-learning (COP-Q), a safety-first method that incorporates inter-objective covariance into vector-valued Q-value estimation. COP-Q constructs a generalized confidence bound in the joint Q-value space and uses Cholesky factorization to encode objective priority in a sequential form. This preserves conservatism on safety while adaptively reducing excessive conservatism on the reward objective. The resulting estimate is used in both temporal-difference target computation and actor optimization. COP-Q incurs minimal computational overhead and is readily compatible with most existing deep Q-learning frameworks. Experiments on robot locomotion in Brax and safe navigation in Safety-Gymnasium, covering both hard- and soft-safety settings, demonstrate that COP-Q achieves strong safety performance together with competitive or improved sample efficiency relative to representative baselines.

SENTINEL combines geometry-based scan statistics with cross - modal depth consistency between LiDAR and an RGB - D camera to compute a per - scan reliability score between 0 and 1. When the score falls below a threshold, corrupted scans are rejected and the robot falls back to calibrated wheel odometry, preventing silent SLAM corruption. Specifically, WAM-Nav utilizes a shared Diffusion Transformer for asymmetric joint diffusion to concurrently generate long-horizon actions and short-horizon visual foresight, reducing the inference latency and visual error accumulation inherent in multi-step autoregressive rollouts. Abstract: Scaling robust robot policies requires more than broader randomization, because physical-domain experience must remain organized and learnable throughout training. We study when a policy can benefit from harder physics and identify recoverability as a central constraint in on-policy physical-domain scaling. Using quadruped locomotion as a physically demanding benchmark for embodied generalization, we introduce HORIZON, a checkpointed frontier curriculum that expands physical domains only within the current policy's recoverable boundary. HORIZON uses rollback and boundary refinement to govern each expansion step, turning fixed randomization into a continual process of physical-domain growth. Experiments reveal three regularities of physical-domain expansion. First, direct domain widening is uneven across physical axes and often unlearnable without staged ordering. Second, domain composition is non-monotonic, and adding more domains beyond a compact core can dilute recoverable joint samples and reduce overall robustness. Third, offline distillation of isolated experts cannot substitute for the joint interaction generated by on-policy curriculum. Together, these results frame physical-domain generalization as a continual growth problem for embodied control, with recoverability as the organizing principle for on-policy expansion.

A purely virtual generation pipeline can provide abundant, varied, and robot-ready data.

Method: GRAIL starts from fully specified 3D configurations (object geometry, camera parameters, metric scale, environment depth, and robot-proportioned character). It uses video foundation models to synthesize interactions in virtual scenes and applies model-based tracking and interaction-aware optimization to reconstruct metric 4D HOI trajectories. Recovered motions are retargeted to a humanoid robot, and complementary trackers (an object-aware latent adaptor and a scene-aware terrain tracker) are trained. The pipeline produces over 20,000 sequences of pick-up, manipulation, sitting, and terrain traversal data, enabling a sim-to-real pipeline.

Result: Egocentric policies trained solely on GRAIL data deployable on a Unitree G1 achieve 84% success on diverse object pick-up and 90% on stair-climbing in real-world tests.

Conclusion: GRAIL demonstrates scalable, robot-ready synthetic data generation for robust loco-manipulation policies, reducing reliance on physical demos and enabling effective sim-to-real transfer across objects and terrains.

Abstract: Scaling humanoid loco-manipulation requires robot-compatible demonstrations across diverse objects, whole-body motions, and scene geometries, but teleoperation and motion capture are difficult to scale because each collection depends on physical setups, instrumented actors, and robot operation. We present GRAIL, a digital generation pipeline that remains fully virtual until deployment: it composes 3D assets, simulator-ready scenes, and priors from video foundation models (VFMs) to synthesize interactions without rebuilding physical environments or teleoperating the robot. Rather than reconstructing unconstrained in-the-wild videos, GRAIL starts from fully specified 3D configurations in which object geometry, camera parameters, metric scale, environment depth, and a robot-proportioned character are known before video generation and reused during reconstruction. This privileged setup better conditions 4D recovery, allowing model-based object tracking, human motion estimation, and interaction-aware optimization to reconstruct metric 4D human-object interaction (HOI) trajectories with reduced depth ambiguity and morphology mismatch. We retarget the recovered motions to a humanoid robot and train complementary task-general trackers: an object-aware latent adaptor for manipulation and a scene-aware tracker for terrain traversal. GRAIL produces over 20,000 sequences spanning pick-up, object manipulation, sitting, and terrain traversal. Using only GRAIL-generated data, we train egocentric visual policies through a sim-to-real pipeline and deploy them on a Unitree G1 humanoid, achieving 84\% real-world success on diverse object pick-up and 90\% success on stair-climbing.

</details>

Our approach first learns deformation-aware visual representations via temporal contrastive pretraining, after which a flow-matching transformer policy conditioned on the demonstration predicts actions to execute the intended manipulation mode. ### [170] [VISTA: Vision-Grounded and Physics-Validated Adaptation of UMI data for VLA Training](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04708)
*Siyuan Yang,Linzheng Guo,Ouyang Lu,Zhaxizhuoma,Daoran Zhang,Xinmiao Wang,Ting Xiao,Fangzheng Yan,Zhijun Chen,Yan Ding,Chao Yu,Chenjia Bai,Xuelong Li*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Scalable real-world robot data with UMI is undermined by two key mismatches: (i) wrist-mounted fisheye views are out-of-distribution for pretrained VLMs due to distortion and gripper-centric perspectives, and (ii) human trajectories often violate kinematic limits, cause collisions, or exceed controller bandwidth, making learned policies infeasible in deployment. A unified approach is needed to align perception and ensure data feasibility for VLA models.

Method: 1) UMI-VQA: a large-scale VQA dataset tailored to wrist-mounted fisheye observations to align VLM representations to distorted visuals via auxiliary vision-language supervision. 2) Physical-validation pipeline: data-completeness pre-check and trajectory scoring for continuity, self-collision risk, and execution fidelity before training. 3) Two-stage co-training: jointly learn vision-language grounding on UMI-VQA and action prediction on validated trajectories.

Result: Empirical results show that incorporating UMI-VQA improves downstream policy performance; physical-validation scores strongly predict deployment success. Across diverse simulation and real-world manipulation tasks, VISTA outperforms strong baselines including π0.5, LingBot-VLA, and Wall-X. Abstract: Universal Manipulation Interface (UMI) enables scalable real-world robot data collection without hardware-specific teleoperation, yet leveraging UMI data to train large-scale Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models remains fundamentally challenging. We identify two critical mismatches: wrist-mounted fisheye views, with severe radial distortion and local gripper-centric perspectives, are out-of-distribution for pretrained VLMs; and human-collected trajectories frequently violate kinematic limits, incur collisions, or exceed controller bandwidth, teaching VLA policies physically infeasible actions. To address the challenges, we present VISTA, a framework that bridges this dual gap through three synergistic components. (i)~UMI-VQA, the first large-scale VQA dataset tailored to wrist-mounted fisheye observations, aligns VLM representations to the distorted visual regime via auxiliary vision-language supervision. (ii)~A systematic physical-validation pipeline performs a data-completeness pre-check and scores each valid trajectory for trajectory continuity, self-collision risk, and execution fidelity before it enters training. (iii)~A two-stage co-training recipe jointly learns vision-language grounding on UMI-VQA and action prediction on validated trajectories. Our experiments empirically show that incorporating UMI-VQA consistently improves downstream policy performance, and that physical-validation scores are strongly predictive of deployment success. On diverse simulation and real-world manipulation tasks, VISTA significantly outperforms strong baselines including $π_{0.5}$, LingBot-VLA, and Wall-X. Results: The dataset comprised 1,641 baseline subjects (608 NC, 767 MCI, 266 AD). On the held-out test set (n = 247), macro AUC was 0.982 (95% CI: 0.965--0.995), accuracy 0.943, balanced accuracy 0.932, macro F1 0.927, and Cohen's kappa 0.909. SHAP analysis identified CDR Global as the dominant predictor for NC and MCI, while CDR-SB and MMSE together drove AD classification. Conclusion: An explainable machine learning model trained on routine clinical assessments achieves near-perfect three-class Alzheimer's detection. ### [172] [Novel Aspects of IEEE SA P3109 Arithmetic Formats for Machine Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04028)
*Andrew Fitzgibbon,Christoph M. Wintersteiger,Jeffrey Sarnoff*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A draft IEEE P3109 proposal for parameterized, ML-friendly binary FP formats with tunable width, precision, sign, and infinity support; defines ops via decoding to extended reals, supports rounding, saturation, stochastic rounding, and NaN handling; includes scale-factor blocks, a vendor-friendly kappa-approximation, and formal verification tooling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To provide compact, consistent numeric representations across diverse bitwidths for ML workloads, enabling predictable semantics, high throughput, and a formal verification path for correctness and interoperability.

Method: Define a family of FP formats parameterized by width, precision, sign, and infinity support; specify arithmetic by mapping operands to closed extended reals and performing real arithmetic; incorporate extensive rounding/saturation modes, stochastic rounding, and exception-free operation with NaN/inf semantics; introduce block operations with shared scale factors; propose a scale-invariant kappa-approximation for vendors; formalize definitions and generate standard functions via formal specifications.

Result: A formalized, verifiable FP format family with tooling for specification, generation, and analysis; includes mechanisms for stochastic rounding, NaN handling, and a novel kappa-approximation to describe approximate implementations.

Conclusion: The draft offers a scalable, verifiable framework for ML-oriented FP formats, promising portable hardware/software semantics and higher throughput, while highlighting the need for careful attention to interoperability, hardware realization of the extended-real semantics, and the practical impact of kappa-approximation and NaN handling on ML accuracy.

Abstract: The IEEE P3109 draft standard defines a parameterized family of binary floating-point formats and associated operations, with a focus on facilitating machine learning. These formats allow efficient and consistent representation of values in a small number of bits. The defined formats are parameterized over width and precision in bits, signedness, and the presence of infinities. Operations are defined by decoding floating-point values to the set of closed extended reals: the reals augmented with positive and negative infinity and NaN (Not a Number). Explicit treatment of NaN and infinite operands ensures that only real arithmetic is invoked in operation definitions. Extensive rounding and saturation modes are defined; stochastic rounding is included. Operations are exception-free, accelerating throughput, with exceptional situations communicated through return values, e.g., NaN. Operations on blocks of values sharing a common scale factor are defined in terms of the underlying operations in a uniform manner. System vendors may describe approximate implementations via a novel scale-invariant measure, akin to units in the last place, called kappa-approximation. Standard function definitions and various other properties are mechanically verified and generated using formal specifications.

Abstract: Reinforcement Learning (RL) has received increasing attention and adoption in real-world use cases. Most of these systems follow a train-then-fix paradigm, where trained agents do not learn while interacting with the world until performance degrades and retraining becomes necessary. In this position paper, we argue that deploying an agent that is incapable of optimality, but receives an evaluative reward signal, is inherently a continual RL problem. We identify four sources of non-stationarity after deployment that necessitate never-ending learning, and highlight why the best deployed agents never stop adapting. We analyze successful examples of continual RL in the real world, and present the community with the advantages and measures to move away from the current train-then-fix paradigm.

### [174] [Do Transformers Need Three Projections? Systematic Study of QKV Variants](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04032)
*Ali Kayyam,Anusha Madan Gopal,M Anthony Lewis*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Projection sharing in Transformers via Q-K=V, Q=K-V, or Q=K=V; Q-K=V preserves performance with substantial KV-cache reductions; 2D encodings address symmetry; enables on-device inference with high memory savings; code released.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand the contribution of the Q, K, and V projections in attention and the impact of sharing (including symmetric vs asymmetric attention) on model performance and memory efficiency, with a focus on edge deployment.

Method: Systematic evaluation across synthetic tasks, vision datasets (MNIST, CIFAR, TinyImageNet, anomaly), and language modeling with 300M and 1.2B parameter models trained on 10B tokens. Compare Q-K=V, Q=K-V, and Q=K=V projections; address symmetric attention via 2D positional encodings; analyze interaction with head sharing (GQA/MQA); measure perplexity and KV/attention cache reductions; study representation spaces and low-rank properties.

Result: Q-K=V performs on par or better than QKV transformer in many settings; achieves 50% KV-cache reduction with only 3.1% perplexity degradation in language modeling; combining Q-K=V with GQA-4 yields 87.5% cache reduction, with MQA yields 96.9%; Q=K-V leads to symmetric attention maps and breaks attention directionality; keys/values can occupy similar representations and attention operates in a low-rank regime; projection sharing is a form of weight tying with direct inference memory benefits, especially for edge deployment.

Conclusion: Projection sharing is a viable, complementary weight-tying strategy in attention, enabling substantial memory and inference efficiency with minimal quality loss; when combined with appropriate head sharing, it enables practical on-device transformers; code is publicly available.

Abstract: Transformers have become the standard solution for various AI tasks, with the query, key, and value (QKV) attention formulation playing a central role. However, the individual contribution of these three projections and the impact of omitting some remain poorly understood. We systematically evaluate three projection sharing constraints: a) Q-K=V (shared key-value), b) Q=K-V (shared query-key), and c) Q=K=V (single projection). The last two variants produce symmetric attention maps; to address this, we also explore asymmetric attention via 2D positional encodings. Through experiments spanning synthetic tasks, vision (MNIST, CIFAR, TinyImageNet, anomaly), and language modeling (300M and 1.2B parameter models on 10B tokens), we discovered that our transformers perform on par or occasionally better than the QKV transformer. In language modeling, Q-K=V projection sharing achieves 50% KV cache reduction with only 3.1% perplexity degradation. Crucially, projection sharing is complementary to head sharing (GQA/MQA): combining Q-K=V with GQA-4 yields 87.5% cache reduction, while Q-K=V + MQA achieves 96.9%, enabling practical on-device inference. We show that Q-K=V preserves quality because keys and values can occupy similar representational spaces and attention operates in a low-rank regime, whereas Q=K-V breaks attention directionality. Our results systematically characterize projection sharing as an underexplored instance of weight tying in attention, with direct, quantifiable inference memory benefits, particularly valuable for edge deployment. The code is publicly available at https://github.com/anushamadan02/Do-Transformers-Need-3-Projections

</details>


### [175] [Inverse Critical Experiment Design via Gradient Optimization and a Multigroup Attention-Based Neural Network Architecture](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04033)
*Will Savage,Logan Burnett,Dean Price*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Inverse design of critical experiments using a differentiable deep learning surrogate to maximize neutronic similarity c_k; achieves high c_k (up to ~0.978) for TN-LC HALEU configurations via gradient-based design of grid-based geometries with a U-Net + multigroup attention pooling architecture.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To enable high-fidelity validation of advanced nuclear reactor designs by generating critical experiments with strong neutronic similarity to target technologies, addressing limited experimental coverage.

Method: Train a deep neural network on OpenMC-calculated sensitivity vectors for grid-based critical-geometry configurations. Architecture combines a U-Net encoder-decoder with a novel multigroup attention pooling layer to capture spatially varying sensitivity features. The surrogate is differentiable, allowing gradient-based optimization over the entire combinatorial design space by re-assigning materials in the geometry grid to maximize c_k.

Result: Applied to TN-Americas TN-LC cask with HALEU fuel; achieved c_k scores of 0.97757, 0.81324, and 0.93276 for three configurations, demonstrating the approach's ability to produce highly similar experiments and accelerate advanced reactor validation.

The multigroup attention pooling adds interpretability and performance advantages over standard pooling. Further work should address generalization, experimental validation, and computational cost.

Abstract: The validation of advanced nuclear reactor designs and fuel concepts requires critical experiments with high neutronic similarity to the target technology. Neutronic similarity is quantified by the correlation coefficient $c_k$, which captures the shared bias in $k_\text{eff}$ induced by uncertainties in nuclear data. Generally, a $c_k\geq0.9$ is needed for an experiment to be sufficiently similar to a target technology. This work presents a methodology for the inverse design of critical experiments. Deep neural network surrogate modeling and nonparametric gradient optimization are used to generate experiment geometries that maximize $c_k$.
  A deep neural network is trained on OpenMC-calculated sensitivity vectors for grid-based critical experiment geometries. The model architecture combines a U-Net convolutional encoder-decoder with a novel multigroup attention pooling layer, introduced to capture the differing spatial dependencies of sensitivities. Multigroup attention pooling is shown to achieve better performance than traditional pooling, as well as interpretable internal behavior. The differentiability of the surrogate enables gradient-based optimization of the full combinatorial design space, allowing $c_k$ to be maximized by directly changing the material assignment of each position in the geometry grid.
  The method is applied to the validation of the TN-Americas TN-LC transportation cask with HALEU fuel, for which existing critical experiment coverage is limited. The optimization procedure is shown to produce experiment geometries achieving $c_k$ scores of 0.97757, 0.81324, and 0.93276 for three configurations of interest. This approach demonstrates the potential of deep learning and gradient optimization to accelerate the development of advanced nuclear technology.

### [177] [LiftQuant: Continuous Bit-Width LLM via Dimensional Lifting and Projection](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04050)
*Liulu He,XuanAng Liu,Juntao Liu,Taolue Feng,Ting Lu,Chunsheng Gan,Zhiyv Peng,Yuan Du,Huanrui Yang,Yijiang Liu,Li Du*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: LiftQuant enables quasi-continuous, hardware-friendly quantization by projecting a 1-bit lattice from a lifted space to approximate weights, yielding a tunable bit-width and Pareto-optimal deployment; demonstrated by compressing a 70B LLM to 2.4 bits for a 24 GB GPU with superior performance to 2-bit baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Address the deployment gap from rigid, integer bit-widths that prevent optimal fitting of large language models to fixed memory budgets; need flexible, Pareto-optimal quantization.

Method: A lift-then-project mechanism: scale a high-dimensional 1-bit lattice into a lower-dimensional weight space, so the effective bit-width equals lifted_dim/orig_dim. Produces a structured, non-uniform codebook approximating vectors with VQ-like expressiveness. Decoding relies on linear transforms and 1-bit uniform quantizers, enabling hardware-friendly operation.

Result: Compression of a 70B LLM to 2.4 bits enabling fit within a 24 GB GPU, with performance surpassing state-of-the-art 2-bit models on the same device.

Conclusion: LiftQuant offers quasi-continuous bit-width control and Pareto-optimal deployment for large models, balancing compression, performance, and hardware practicality; code and checkpoints released.

Abstract: Existing quantization methods are fundamentally limited by rigid, integer-based bit-widths (e.g., 2, 3-bit), resulting in a ``deployment gap" where Large Language Models cannot be optimally fitted to specific memory budgets. To bridge this gap, we introduce LiftQuant, a novel framework that enables continuous bit-width control for true Pareto-optimal deployment. The core innovation is a ``lift-then-project" mechanism which approximates low-dimensional weight vectors by projecting a simple 1-bit lattice from a higher-dimensional ``lifted" space. Crucially, the effective bit-width is determined simply by the ratio of the lifted dimension to the original dimension, which allows the bit-width to be tuned quasi-continuous as the dimension is a flexible structural parameter. This projection generates a structured yet non-uniform codebook, capturing the expressive power of Vector Quantization (VQ). While beneficial over VQ, LiftQuant's decoding path relies solely on linear transformations and 1-bit uniform quantizers, retaining hardware-friendly nature. This flexibility is transformative: LiftQuant enables a 70B LLM to be compressed to 2.4 bits to precisely fit a 24GB GPU, where its performance significantly surpasses state-of-the-art 2-bit models fitted on the same device. Our code and ckpt is available at https://github.com/Heliulu/LiftQuant.

</details>


### [178] [A Goal-Set Characterization of Task Composition in the Boolean Task Algebra](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04053)
*Eduardo Terrés-Caballero,Herke van Hoof*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: In deterministic MDPs, optimal extended Q-functions under the Boolean Task Algebra are fully determined by the universal and empty tasks, making the original logarithmically-sized base-task set redundant; a goal-set-based composition method reconstructs values from universal/empty, reducing learning and composition costs without sacrificing performance. <details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Clarify the assumptions and efficiency of zero-shot task composition via BTA, reduce learning and computation costs, and test whether a smaller base-task set suffices across diverse domains.

Method: Reanalyze BTA to prove a collapse in deterministic MDPs; introduce a goal-set-based composition that operates on goal sets and reconstructs composed value functions from universal and empty value functions; compare with existing BTA and Skill Machines across tabular, visual, function-approximation, and continuous-control domains; provide a stochastic-counterexample illustrating the limits of collapse.

Result: Deterministic setting: optimal extended Q-functions are determined by universal and empty tasks, making the base-task set redundant and enabling cost reductions in learning and composition; empirical results show learning extra base tasks does not improve performance; stochastic setting may invalidate the collapse, with a counterexample showing the need to account for exponentially many policies; code available at the provided GitHub URL.

Conclusion: The collapse is contingent on determinism; a lean base task set suffices for deterministic BTA-like composition, improving efficiency; in stochastic environments, the original complexity may be unavoidable, necessitating consideration of a larger policy space.

Abstract: The Boolean Task Algebra (BTA) provides a principled framework for zero-shot task composition in reinforcement learning by equipping goal-reaching tasks with Boolean operations. We revisit its structural assumptions and formalize a collapse in the space of optimal extended Q-value functions: in deterministic MDPs, every such function is fully determined by the universal and empty tasks. This makes the logarithmic set of base tasks proposed in the original BTA formulation redundant. Building on this observation, we introduce a goal-set-based composition method that performs logical operations on goal sets and reconstructs composed value functions by selecting slices from the universal and empty value functions. This reduces learning costs for standard BTA and reduces composition time for both BTA and Skill Machines, while preserving policy performance. Experiments across tabular, visual, function-approximation, and continuous-control domains show that learning additional base tasks does not yield better performance. Finally, we study the stochastic setting and provide a counterexample showing that this collapse need not hold, that is, optimal composition may require accounting for exponentially many policies in the number of goals. Code is available at https://github.com/EduardoTerres/bta_paper.

### [179] [Spectral Scaling Laws of Muon](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04058)
*Gagik Magakyan,Pablo Parrilo,Asuman Ozdaglar*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: Empirical study of Muon's Newton–Schulz orthonormalization shows layer- and model-size–dependent stabilization of momentum singular-value spectra, enabling a layer-aware NS configuration that reduces cost while preserving update quality; early layers scale mildly with model size, while some late layers require more NS iterations to avoid orthonormalization failure as models grow.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how the NS approximation in Muon's orthonormalization behaves across training and scales with model size, and to provide guidance for setting NS iterations per layer to balance computational cost and update quality.

Method: Systematic measurement of singular-value quantiles of momentum buffers across layers in models from 77M to 2.8B parameters. Analyze stabilization values, fit power laws with layer-dependent exponents, and assess the sufficiency of a fixed NS configuration (e.g., 5 steps) across scales.

Result: After a short burn-in, momentum-spectral quantiles stabilize at layer-type- and size-dependent values that follow clean power laws in model size with layer-specific exponents. Mid-to-late-depth layers scale weakly with model size (~M^{-0.25}) so a 5-step NS remains effective at large scales, while some late layers scale strongly (up to ~M^{-0.96}) and may enter NS failure unless more NS steps or better-tuned coefficients are used. These laws enable a principled, layer-aware NS configuration that minimizes unnecessary computation while preserving update quality.

Conclusion: The study provides a principled framework to adapt NS iterations per layer as model size grows, enabling efficient orthonormalization in large LMs and highlighting heterogeneity across layers in spectral scaling.

Abstract: Orthonormalized update rules have rapidly become a leading choice of optimizer for training large language models, with recent open-source state-of-the-art models adopting Muon. To keep these updates tractable, Muon performs the orthonormalization with the Newton--Schulz (NS) iteration. Since NS is only approximate, directions with small singular values fail to be orthonormalized. In Muon, NS is applied to the momentum matrix at every step, yet little is known about how the singular value spectrum of these momentum matrices behaves during training, or how that behavior changes with model size. We present the first systematic study of this question. Tracking singular value quantiles of the momentum buffer across layers in models ranging from 77M to 2.8B parameters, we observe a consistent picture: after a short burn-in, the quantiles stabilize at a value determined by the layer type and model size. These stabilization values follow remarkably clean power laws in model size, with layer-dependent exponents. Layers up to mid-late depth scale very mildly with model size $M$ (around $M^{-0.25}$), so the standard 5-step NS configuration used at academic scale will continue to orthonormalize them at much larger scales. Some of the late layers, however, scale much more aggressively (up to $M^{-0.96}$) and will fall into the NS failure regime at frontier scale unless one uses more NS iterations or better-tuned coefficients. NS iterations are computationally expensive at scale; our laws give practitioners a principled, layer-aware recipe for choosing the minimum NS configuration that still orthonormalizes the directions that matter -- avoiding unnecessary computation without sacrificing update quality.

### [180] [Adaptive Patching Is Harder Than It Looks For Time-Series Forecasting](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04074)
*Federico Zucchi,Yi Xie,Chao Zhang,Keyuan Luo,Thomas Lampert,Ziyue Li*

Main category: cs.LG

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To determine when content-adaptive patching justifies its complexity by formalizing its benefits and deriving conditions under which it outperforms uniform patching in time-series forecasting.

Method: Model patching as a budgeted bitrate allocation, derive an explicit threshold a dynamic patching rule must satisfy to beat a tuned uniform baseline, establish local (quadratic) and global (strong-convexity) bounds on potential improvements, prove two structural results about local complexity and representation-alignment, and validate via controlled-isolation experiments across three architectures comparing adaptive patching to uniform patch-size sweeps on standard long-horizon forecasting benchmarks.

Result: A threshold condition for outperforming the uniform baseline; local quadratic and global strong-convexity bounds on improvement; structural results: (1) without coupling, scalar local complexity cannot yield a non-uniform optimum; (2) once the backbone attains representation-aware optimum, alignment gains collapse near a uniform patch size; empirically, the uniform baseline is competitive and most gains are dataset/method-specific; no consistent directional advantage of adaptivity when results are aggregated by dataset.

Conclusion: Adaptive patching should be benchmarked against a tuned uniform baseline; its value depends on the reliability of a routing signal that identifies where finer patches truly reduce forecasting loss.

Abstract: Adaptive patching is a recent and compelling proposal for time-series Transformers: allocate finer patches where the sequence looks locally informative. This paper asks under what conditions a content-adaptive patching operator should outperform a tuned uniform one. Local heterogeneity alone is not enough: under pointwise forecasting losses, a complex-looking region is not automatically one where finer patching reduces the loss. We model patching as a budgeted bitrate allocation and derive an explicit threshold that a dynamic patching rule must satisfy to beat a well-tuned uniform baseline, then bound the achievable improvement both locally (a quadratic surrogate) and globally (a strong-convexity bound under the model's assumptions). Two structural results follow: without a coupling constraint, scalar local complexity cannot produce a non-uniform optimum under a common loss landscape; and once the backbone is trained to its representation-aware optimum, the alignment gain collapses around a well-tuned uniform patch size. To test these predictions, we run a controlled isolation study on three representative architectures, replacing each adaptive mechanism with a uniform patch-size sweep while keeping the backbone, data, and training protocol fixed. On standard long-horizon forecasting benchmarks, the validation-selected uniform baseline is competitive with the dynamic counterpart, with per-setting effects concentrated near zero and no consistent directional advantage once results are aggregated by dataset. The larger gains we do observe are method- and dataset-specific. Adaptive patching should therefore be evaluated against a tuned uniform baseline; its value depends on whether a cheap and reliable routing signal can identify where finer patches actually reduce forecasting loss.

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: FolT-MCMC is a method for Bayesian posteriors with finite symmetry (label switching) that operates on the quotient space to remove redundant multimodality. It uses an independence sampler with a quotient proposal built by symmetrising a learned normalising flow over group orbits, and provides theoretical certifiable guarantees that transfer from the unfolded to the quotient metric. Empirically it shows large improvements in certified bounds (2x–145x) across Gaussian mixtures and yields non-vacuous certificates on real data where unfolded methods fail.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Posterior multimodality induced by symmetry (e.g., label-switching in mixture models) degrades MCMC convergence and inference. Traditional MCMC traverses equivalent modes related by a permutation group, leading to inefficient mixing and unreliable diagnostics. The aim is to perform inference directly on the quotient posterior (identifying symmetric components) and to obtain certified convergence guarantees.

Method: Construct an independence sampler targeting the quotient posterior on the fundamental domain of the symmetry group. The quotient proposal is formed by symmetrising a learned normalising flow over the group orbits (a folded normalising flow). Theoretical results establish transfer of the LCNF oscillation-based certification framework to the quotient metric, with a stabiliser-corrected ball-mass bound and improved covering radius. The framework yields a quantile-core certified lower bound that improves when the unfolded flow has cross-mode proposal deficiency.

Conclusion: FolT-MCMC effectively mitigates symmetry-induced posterior multimodality by performing inference on the quotient, enabling stronger certified guarantees and improved convergence diagnostics. It scales to higher dimensions and a larger number of modes, and applies to real-world data where traditional methods fail to provide meaningful certificates.

Abstract: Bayesian models with finite symmetry - mixture models with exchangeable components, structural identification with closely-spaced modes - define posteriors that are invariant under a group of label permutations, creating redundant multimodality that degrades MCMC convergence diagnostics. We introduce Folded Transport MCMC (FolT-MCMC), which performs inference directly on the quotient posterior by constructing an independence sampler on the fundamental domain of the symmetry group. The quotient proposal is formed by symmetrising a learned normalising flow over the group orbits. We prove that the LCNF oscillation-based certification framework transfers to the quotient metric with a stabiliser-corrected ball-mass bound and improved covering radius, and that the quantile-core certified lower bound improves whenever the unfolded flow exhibits cross-mode proposal deficiency. On Gaussian mixtures (d = 2 - 20), label-switching targets (up to 24 equivalent modes), and a standard Bayesian three-component mixture posterior, the quantile-core certified improvement ratio ranges from 2x to 145x, with the folded certificate empirically nearly dimension-free. On real accelerometer data from a supertall building during Typhoon Mangkhut, FolT-MCMC yields a non-vacuous quantile-core certificate where the unfolded certificate is vacuous.

### [191] [Bayes-Sufficient Representations in Supervised Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2606.04045)
*Vasileios Sevetlidis*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: A formal framework for task-specific representation learning using Bayes-sufficient representations. It introduces the Bayes quotient as the minimal object that partitions inputs by requiring the same Bayes-optimal action; sufficiency/refinement vs minimality/equivalence characterize when a representation preserves or reduces needed information for a fixed loss. The paper connects to property elicitation and demonstrates distinctions through controlled, neural-bottleneck, and real-data experiments on iNaturalist taxonomy refinement.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To redefine what 'relevant information' means for representations in a fixed supervised decision problem, making relevance depend on the loss and the Bayes-optimal action, and to unify information-theoretic and decision-theoretic views via the Bayes quotient and property elicitation.

Method: Formal framework: a representation is Bayes-sufficient for a joint distribution and loss if some prediction head can implement a Bayes-optimal action rule. The Bayes quotient identifies inputs requiring the same Bayes-optimal action. A representation is sufficient when it refines this quotient, and Bayes-minimal when it is informationally equivalent to it. The framework links to property elicitation (zero-one loss → Bayes class, squared loss → conditional mean, Brier → conditional probability, log loss → predictive distribution). Empirical validation via controlled finite experiments, learned neural bottlenecks, and a real-data iNaturalist taxonomic refinement task.

Result: Key results: the distribution and loss determine the Bayes action, the action determines the Bayes quotient, and the quotient determines the minimal information needed for Bayes-optimal prediction. Sufficiency means the representation preserves the quotient structure (refines it); minimality means informational equivalence to the quotient. The experiments illustrate the distinctions between sufficiency, minimality, and retained non-required information across tasks and losses.

Conclusion: The framework provides a principled, loss-aware target for representation learning: identify the Bayes action for the task, derive the Bayes quotient, and compress inputs to keep only information that affects the Bayes-optimal decision. This clarifies when information can be discarded and links representation learning to property elicitation; practical methods such as bottleneck architectures can be guided by the quotient rather than generic information preservation.

Abstract: Representation learning is often described as preserving the information in an input that is relevant for prediction. This work asks what relevance means for a fixed supervised decision problem. A representation is defined to be Bayes-sufficient for a joint distribution and loss if some prediction head can use it to implement a Bayes-optimal action rule. This makes the target information loss-dependent. In the almost-surely unique Bayes-action case, the relevant object is a Bayes quotient, which identifies inputs that require the same Bayes-optimal action. A representation is sufficient when it refines this quotient, and Bayes-minimal when it is informationally equivalent to it. The framework connects naturally to property elicitation: zero-one loss requires the Bayes class, squared loss the conditional mean, Brier loss the conditional probability in binary prediction, and log loss or strictly proper scoring rules the predictive distribution. Controlled finite experiments, learned neural bottleneck experiments, and a real-data iNaturalist taxonomic refinement experiment illustrate the distinction between sufficiency, minimality, and retained non-required information. For a fixed supervised problem, the distribution and the loss determine the Bayes action, the Bayes action determines the quotient, and the quotient determines the minimal information required for Bayes-optimal prediction.

Model side: we show that RT performs relation-level ICL, and a kernel regression view shows it fails when sparse label-cell coverage yields an underdetermined regression. Data side: we ablate RT's pre-training source and find that existing synthetic-only pre-training and in-distribution pre-training drive the same architecture into different regimes, lazy vs. feature-learning. Probing this gap reveals that the missing ingredient is a support-identifiable relational latent in the label-generation process.
